[3] The Death of a Man
[4] The first rule that every magician must remember is: never tell the audience what you're going to do.
[5] Once you do, you immediately give them a direction for their alertness, which is the last thing you want to do;
[6] At the same time, it also increases the chances of being caught by ten times.
[7] Let's take an example.
[8] - Professor Hoffman: "Modern Magic"
[9] Chapter 1
[10] In Kent County, Brian Keyes sat at a window overlooking the garden, facing a desk cluttered with open books, with a strong aversion to work rising in his heart. The mid-July sun filtered through the two windows, turning the room floor into a golden hue. The heat that made one drowsy wafted a smell of old wood and ancient books. A wasp buzzed in from the apple orchard behind the garden, and Keyes lazily waved it away.
[11] Beyond the garden wall, a quarter-mile-long road winds through the orchards to the Bull's Head Inn. The road passes by the gates of Bly House - Pike can see many of the house's tall chimneys poking through the trees - and then climbs over the "Picturesque" woodland.
[12] The flat, light brown-green landscape of Kent, normally devoid of bright colors, now shines brilliantly. Pike imagines that even the chimneys of Bly House have taken on color. On the road, Mr. Nattie Barrow's car comes from the direction of Bly House, and the sound can be heard from a distance, although the speed is not fast.
[13] McLinford is already a restless place, Brian Pike thinks despondently. If someone thinks this is an exaggeration, he can provide evidence. Last summer, there was a murder case in which the beautiful Miss Dare was strangled by a vagrant, who later died trying to escape across the railway. And just last week, in the last week of July, two strangers stayed at the Bull's Head Inn: one was an artist, and the other was reportedly a detective - no one knows how this rumor spread.
[14] Today, Pike's friend, the lawyer Nattie Barrow from Meadon, also makes mysterious trips back and forth. Something seems to be happening at Bly House, although no one knows what it is. Brian Pike is used to going to the Bull's Head Inn for a beer and lunch at noon; but this morning there is an unusual phenomenon, that is, there is not a single rumor in the inn.
[15] Pike stretches and pushes a few books aside. He idly wonders what could be happening at Bly House? Since Sir Nigel Chawker built the building during the reign of King James I, there has been no commotion. The Blay family has been known for its prosperity and is still a prolific family. The current Earl of Blay, Sir John Blay, is the heir to a substantial fortune and fertile estates.
[16] Casby likes the dark-haired, somewhat neurotic John Armingray and his wife, the spirited and forthright Miss Irene. Life in Armingray suits him well; he has adapted well; he is a natural-born landowner, despite being away from home for a long time. Another reason Casby finds Armingray interesting is his romantic history, which is now difficult to associate with the serious and almost ordinary baronet at Armingray Manor. From less than a year ago, when he first went out to marry Miss Irene Sudden, to now, it has been another opportunity to bring vitality to Mallingford village.
[17] Casby furrows his brow and stretches again, picking up a pen. It's time to get to work.
[18] Oh, dear.
[19] He looks at the paper on his elbow - "The Lives of Leading Figures in the British Legal World," an article he is trying to write in a way that is accessible to both experts and laypeople, which is becoming increasingly interesting. He is currently working on the chapter about Sir Maurice Hall. But there are always various distractions that come and go, because distractions just happen, and Brian Casby does not want to exclude them.
[20] To be honest, he never expected to finish "The Lives of Leading Figures in the British Legal World," as he was not as enthusiastic about getting a law degree. He is too lazy to be a real scholar, but too intellectual and curious to give up. Whether he can finish the article on the leaders of the legal world is not important. But he can use it as a reminder to work harder, so he can then enjoy wandering down the interesting side paths.
[21] There is a pamphlet next to him that says that a witch tour was held in Suffolk on March 10, 1664, presided over by Sir Matthew Hale, who was then the Chief Justice of the Court of Exchequer in Kent. The pamphlet was published in 1718 due to Brown, Watson, and Wadton.
[22] This is one of the paths he has wandered into. Of course, the relationship between Sir Matthew Hale and the witches is trivial. But it doesn't matter to Brian Bai to devote half a chapter to writing about any topic he is interested in. He happily took an old "Granville" from the bookshelf. Just as he was about to immerse himself in it, he heard footsteps in the courtyard and someone shouted "hey" at the window.
[23] It's Nathanael Barrow, shaking a suitcase, not looking like a lawyer at all.
[24] "Busy?" Barrow asked.
[25] "Well," Baiji replied, yawning and putting down the "Granville," "come in for a smoke."
[26] Barrow pushed open the glass door leading to the courtyard and entered the dimly lit, comfortable room. Despite his efforts to remain calm, he couldn't hide his excitement, which made him seem chilly and pale on this hot afternoon. His father, grandfather, and great-grandfather had all handled the legal affairs of the Purnell family. Sometimes it was hard not to wonder if Nathaniel Barrow's impatience and occasional outbursts made him the right choice to be the family lawyer; after all, he was still young. However, he was competent and in control; and besides, Chegwin thought, he could put on a face as cold as a slab of sole on a chopping board.
[27] Barrow's dark hair had a broad parting that smoothly circled his forehead. He wore a pair of shell-rimmed spectacles over his long nose, and his facial muscles seemed more pronounced than usual. He was dressed in a well-made but uncomfortable black suit and wore gloves with his hands clenching a briefcase.
[28] "Bryan," he said, "are you planning to have dinner at home tonight?"
[29] "Yes, I –"
[30] "No!" Barrow exclaimed suddenly.
[31] Barnaby blinked.
[32] "You must go to the Pembroke house for dinner," Barrow continued. "Frankly, I don't care if you go to the Pembroke house for dinner, but I hope you'll be there when things happen." He resumed his serious demeanor, standing with his thin chest puffed out. "What I'm about to tell you is authorized. Fortunately, I ask you: have you ever considered the possibility that Sir John Pembroke might not be a man of integrity?"
[33] "Integrity?"
[34] "Perhaps Sir John Pembroke is a fraud, an imposter," Barrow cautiously explained. "Maybe he's not even Sir John Pembroke?"
[35] "Have you gone mad?" Barnaby sat up straight, both surprised and angry, and inexplicably uneasy. It was not a good time to be angry on such a hot day, especially during the laziest part of the day. "Of course, I never thought of such a thing. What's gotten into you?"
[36] Nathan Barro stood up and put his briefcase on the chair.
[37] "Well, I mean," he replied, "someone suddenly came claiming to be the real Sir John Fang Lei. It's not news, it's been going on for months, and now it's getting serious. Er... " He hesitated, looking around. "Is anyone else in the house? That lady... you know, the one who takes care of your household... or anyone else?"
[38] "No."
[39] Barro's words came out in a strained manner. "I shouldn't tell you this, but I know I can trust you; besides, my situation is quite embarrassing right now, and it could cause trouble. Compared to the Ditchburn case, it's really trivial. Of course... er... on the surface, I have no reason to doubt that my employer is not Sir John Fang Lei. I should serve the real Sir John Fang Lei. But the problem is here. There are now two sirs, one the true baronet and the other a counterfeit. These two people are completely different; they don't even look alike. But if I can't tell who's who, it will be disastrous."
[40] Paochi was busy adjusting his thoughts. He pushed the cigarette box towards Barro, lit a cigarette for himself, and looked at the visitor.
[41] "This is quite a revelation," he said. "What makes you think there's a fraud involved? Have you ever suspected anything before?"
[42] "Never. Let me tell you why.” Barrow took out a handkerchief and wiped his face carefully before sitting back calmly. "I wish it were all just a rumor. I really like John and Iris – sorry, I mean Sir John and Lady Farrar – I really like them both. If that guy turns out to be a fraud, I'll be happy to dance on the grass in the village – well, maybe not, perhaps – but I'll make sure he gets a longer sentence for perjury than Arthur Olden. Moreover, since we're about to uncover the truth tonight, it's best for you to understand the whole story and the origins of this messy situation. Do you know John Farrar's life story?"
[43] "Not very well."
[44] "You can't know anything half-heartedly," Barrow shook his head disapprovingly. "Do you write history stories like that? I hope not. Listen carefully and remember all the details."
[45] "We have to go back 25 years, to when John Farrar, the second son of Sir Dudley Farrar and Lady Farrar, was 15 years old. He was born in 1898 and had no chance of inheriting the title, as his older brother, Sir Dudley, was the favorite son of Sir Dudley and Lady Farrar."
[46] "They demanded that their son be of high moral character. Sir Dudley Aramayne (I've known him for most of my life) was a man of the Victorian era and of the very strict type. Although not as exaggerated as some of the legends describe, I remember being surprised by the 6 pence silver coin he gave me when I was a child. "
[47] "Young Dudley was a good boy, but John was not. He had dark hair, was quiet, uninhibited, but gloomy, and any slight misbehavior was enough to make people dislike him. He was not really bad, just not fitting in and wanting to be treated like an adult before he grew up. In 1912, at the age of 15, he had an adult-like affair with a hotel waitress in Meadton - "
[48] Baskerville whistled. He glanced out the window, as if expecting Aramayne himself to appear.
[49] "At 15?" Baskerville said. "He must have been a rakehell!"
[50] "Yes."
[51] Baskerville hesitated. "However, you know, based on my observations of Mr. Ramey, I often find him –"
[52] "A bit Puritanical," added Barrow. "Yes, but we're talking about a 15-year-old boy. He was obsessed with the occult, including witchcraft and Satanism, which was bad enough, and he was rejected by Eton. But the public scandal with the hotel waitress was the last straw for his family. Sir Dudley Ramey decided the boy was irredeemable and never wanted to see him again. So they took the usual course of action. Sir Ramey's cousin, who lived in America and was doing well, took John in. John packed his bags and went to America.
[53] "The only one who could control him was a man named Kenney Murrey, a teacher. This man was in his mid-twenties and had a hobby in criminology, which was the reason John went to see him in the first place. This hobby was not common in those days, but Sir Dudley liked and approved of Mr. Murrey, so he didn't say much.
[54] "At that time, Mr. Murrey had just landed a good job as an assistant principal at a school in Hamilton, Bermuda, which required him to leave the estate. He accepted the job; the estate no longer needed him. So Mr. Murrey was entrusted with taking the boy to New York to prevent any trouble along the way. He was to deliver the boy to Sir Ramey's cousin and then board a ship to Bermuda."
[55] Natacha Ramey paused, reminiscing about the past.
[56] "Honestly, I don't remember much about that time," he added. "The adults kept us younger children and that evil John apart. Only little Iris Suddon, who was probably around six or seven years old at the time, was completely devoted to John. She couldn't bear to hear anyone say a bad word about him, and it was only natural that she would marry him. I vaguely remember John taking a carriage to the train station, wearing a straw hat and sitting next to Kenny Murrey. They were supposed to take a ship the next day, and it was a lively day.
[57] Barrow and Pang both recalled the past. Pang remembered it as a chaotic day, with clippings plastered all over the street corners and rumors abound with no basis in fact.
[58] "The supposedly unsinkable Titanic hit an iceberg and sank on April 15, 1912," Barrow continued. "In the chaos, Murrey and the boy were separated. Murrey drifted for 18 hours in icy water with two or three other people, clinging to a wooden grate. They were rescued by a cargo ship, the Corwind, heading to Bermuda. Murrey was taken to his intended destination, and he was relieved to learn that John Fairley was safe and sound, as confirmed by a letter he received soon after.
[59] "John Fairley, or rather a boy who claimed to be named John, was rescued by the Irisca, bound for New York. Lady Fairley's cousin, a westerner, met him. Everything was calm as usual. Sir Dudley Fairley remained hopeful that the boy was still alive, but his pain was no greater than that of the boy. Old Sir Dudley Fairley's suffering was no greater than that of the boy.
[60] "He grew up in America, where he lived for nearly 25 years. He refused to write to his family, and he would only send photos or birthday cards if they died. Fortunately, he became close to his American cousin, Blue Will, and found some satisfaction in his longing for family. He - uh - seemed to change a lot, working as a quiet farmer in a vast field, just as he should have been here. In the later stages of the war, he joined the American army, but he never set foot on British soil or met his relatives. He didn't even see Murrey again. Murrey settled down in Bermuda, but he didn't live a very comfortable life. They both couldn't afford the travel expenses to visit each other, especially since John Fairley was far away in Colorado."
[61] "Back home, everything was as usual. The child was almost forgotten; after his mother's death in 1926, he was completely forgotten. Four years later, his father passed away. Little Dudley - now grown up - inherited the title and all the estates. He never married, saying there was plenty of time; but fate intervened, and in August 1935, the new Sir Dudley died of food poisoning."
[62] Brian Puckle reflected.
[63] "This happened after I arrived here," Puckle said, "but didn't Dudley ever try to get in touch with his younger brother?"
[64] "He did. All his letters were returned unopened. Young Dudley was a stickler for rules, and they were separated during his formative years, so John clearly felt no kinship. However, when the question of whether John should inherit the title and estates after Dudley's death -"
[65] "John accepted."
[66] "He accepted. That's the problem," Barrow said excitedly, "you know him, you know the situation. It seemed perfectly natural for him to come back here, and he didn't seem to feel any unfamiliarity with the place, despite having been away for 25 years. We didn't feel strange about him either, and his thoughts, behavior, and even speech were quite befitting of an heir to the Arlmer title. He returned in early 1936. The romantic episode was his reunion with the grown-up Iris Sudden, whom he married in May of that same year. It's been just over a year since then, and now this has happened. It's unbelievable."
[67] "I guess it's probably that," Pike said uncertainly, "that the confusion over the Titanic disaster mixed up his identity, right? The boy who was rescued was someone else's son, who somehow claimed to be John Arlmer?"
[68] Barrow paced back and forth, tapping his fingers on the passing furniture, but his demeanor was not at all comical. He had a cunning charm that could make his clients feel at ease and even hypnotized. He had a little trick of turning his head to one side and looking at the other through the frames of his large glasses, which he did now.
[69] "Indeed, that's exactly it. Do you know, if John Arlmer in front of you is a fraud, then he's been playing the part since 1912 - and the real heir is nowhere to be found? He's deliberate. When the disaster occurred, he was rescued onto a lifeboat wearing Arlmer's clothes and ring; he had Arlmer's diary. He was abandoned and went to live with his Uncle Blaine in America. He returned here and blended into the past. And, he's been here for 25 years! His handwriting has changed, his face and birthmarks have changed, and even his memories have become hazy. Do you understand the difficulty now? Even if his memory is flawed, even if there are gaps, it's a natural phenomenon, isn't it?"
[70] Pike shook his head.
[71] "Still, the plaintiff must have some strong evidence to prove his case. You know how things work in court. What kind of evidence does he have?"
[72] "This plaintiff, Mr. Barrow, has a powerful piece of evidence that proves he is the rightful John Marley, Jr. Sir."
[73] "Have you seen this evidence?"
[74] "We will see it tonight – or not. The plaintiff has requested a meeting with the current Marley. No, Brian, I am not a simple-minded person, although I am almost driven crazy by this. It's not just because the plaintiff's story is very credible and provides the most minute evidence; it's not just because he walked into my office (and I shouldn't tell you, he brought a tough legal representative with him) and told me things only John Marley himself could know. Again, only John Marley himself. But he also suggested that both he and the current Marley accept a decisive test."
[75] "What test?"
[76] "Watch, we will watch,” Nathan Barrow picked up his briefcase, "there is only one thing to be glad about in this whole mess, and that is that so far, things have not been reported. At least the petitioner is a gentleman – indeed, they both are – he does not want to stir up trouble. But let me sort this out first, then there will be hell to pay."
[77] "What does John, the Earl, think about this?"
[78] "Which John, Earl?"
[79] "For clarity and convenience," Caskin replied, "we will call the one we used to know John Arlery, the Earl of Arlery. It is interesting that you believe the petitioner is the true Earl, isn't it?"
[80] "Not entirely. Of course not!” Barrow straightened up, proud. "Arlery is just – complaining. I think that is a good sign."
[81] "Does Irene know?"
[82] "Yes. The knight told her today. That's it. What I'm telling you is what no lawyer should say, and very few do. But if I can't trust you, there's no one else I can trust. Besides, since my father's death, I've been a bit unsure about my methods. Get into the swing of things and try to understand my plight. You have to be at the Aramray manor at 7 pm. We need you as a witness. You have to observe the two parties and use your wisdom. Then, before we take action," Barro tapped the edge of the briefcase on the table, "you have to tell me who is who."
[83] Note: The Law Tracts of Glanville, a collection of legal documents written by the 12th-century Chief Justice of England, Renaulf de Glanville, commonly referred to as Glanville.
[84] Note: The case of Roger Charles Dyer, considered one of the most famous cases in British history, involved a man named Roger who disappeared in a shipwreck and was presumed dead. His mother advertised in Australia for him, and he was found and returned to England. The court ruled that he was an imposter named Arthur Eaden, and Eaden was convicted of perjury and served a prison sentence until 1884.
[85] Chapter Two
[86] Shadows fell over the sloping hillside under the trees known as "The Screen," but the flat land to the left remained bright and warm. The house, separated from the road by a wall and trees, stood behind it, with a facade that seemed to come from an old painting in deep brick red. It looked elegant and well-maintained, like its well-groomed lawn. The windows were high and narrow, with rectangular stone frames. A straight gravel driveway led to the gate. Several tall chimneys stood next to each other in the twilight.
[87] No ivy dared to climb the front of the building, but there was a grove of beech trees behind it. A row of newer outbuildings extended from the main house, forming a reversed T-shape, dividing the Dutch-style garden in half. On one side of the house, overlooking the garden, was the back window of a room where John Barrow, 1st Baronet, and Lady Barrow were waiting.
[88] The clock in the room ticked. This was a room that had been used as a music room or a ladies' boudoir in the 18th century, reflecting the country it belonged to. There was a piano in the room, its old wood gleaming like a turtle shell; there were also elegant antique silverware. From the north-facing window, one could see the "Screen" landscape; Lady Barrow had been using this room as a living room.
[89] Lady Barrow sat in the shade of a large "octopus-shaped" beech tree at the window. She was the kind of girl who loved the outdoors, with a sturdy and athletic build, a square but attractive face. Her dark brown hair was pulled back into an untidy bun. Her sun-kissed, eager face had warm brown eyes that were honest and direct as a handshake. Her mouth was perhaps a bit wide, but when she laughed, she always showed a row of white teeth. She may not have been a beauty, but her health and vitality gave her an even more intense appeal.
[90] However, she was not laughing now. Her gaze remained fixed on her husband, who was pacing around the room in short steps.
[91] "Are you worried?" she asks.
[92] Sir John Armin suddenly stops, unconsciously turning his two dark wrists, and begins to pace again.
[93] "Worried? No, not at all. Oh, no. It's just – damn it!"
[94] He seems to be the ideal partner for her. If his appearance perfectly matches his title as a gentleman, it may give a false impression, as the title of gentleman was once associated with ruthless brutes, and there is a more realistic model. Armin is of medium height, thin and wiry, reminiscent of a plow blade cutting through the furrows of a field, with a bright metal and sharp blade.
[95] He is around forty years old. He has dark hair and skin, with a dense but short-cut beard. His dark hair is interspersed with strands of gray, and his piercing eyes have developed wrinkles at the corners. He is at the peak of his mental and physical abilities, a man with great explosive power. He paces back and forth in the small room, with more discomfort and embarrassment than anger or sadness.
[96] Lily stood up. She exclaimed:
[97] "Oh, dear, why didn't you tell me sooner?"
[98] "There was no need to burden you with my worries," he said. "It's my problem, and I'll handle it myself."
[99] "How long have you known?"
[100] "A couple of months, I guess."
[101] "Has this been bothering you all this time?" she asked, her eyes showing another kind of worry.
[102] "It's part of the reason," he mumbled, quickly glancing at her.
[103] "What does that mean?"
[104] "That's what I mean. It's part of the reason, dear."
[105] "John...isn't it about Madeline Dan?"
[106] He stopped in his tracks. "Good heavens, no! Of course not. I don't understand why you'd ask that. You really don't like Madeline, do you?"
[107] "I don't like her eyes. They seem a bit eerie. Sorry, I shouldn't have said that. There's enough going on already. It's not pleasant, but it's okay, right? That person doesn't have any grounds, does he?"
[108] "He doesn't have any right. I don't know if he has any grounds."
[109] His tone was abrupt, causing her to look at him closely.
[110] "But why all the trouble and mystery? Since he's a fake, why not just ignore him and let things end?"
[111] "Barrow said it's not wise. Anyway, we have to first—uh—find out his intentions and then come up with a strategy. An effective one. Moreover—"
[112] Lily's face lost its expression.
[113] "I wish I could help you," she said. "I don't think I could contribute much, but I'm curious about what's going on. I know this man is trying to provoke you to prove he's the real you. It's just a silly prank. I've known you for years; I recognized you right away when I saw you again; you might be surprised how easily I recognized you. But I know you brought this man to your house, and Barrow and another lawyer, all mysterious-like. What are you planning to do?"
[114] "Do you remember my tutor, Kenny Murrey?"
[115] "I have a vague recollection," Lily said, frowning. "A rather tall and interesting man with a beard like a sailor and an artist. I thought he was young at the time, but he seemed a bit older. He told some fantastic stories."
[116] "His ambition was to become a great detective," he answered firmly. "He was brought here from Bermuda. He claims he can distinguish the real John Jolary. He's at the Bullough Inn now."
[117] "Wait a minute!" Jolary said. "Everyone in the village is talking about a man staying at the inn, 'looking like an artist.' Is that Murrey?"
[118] "Indeed, it's the old Murrey. I wanted to visit him, but it's a bit - uh, ungentlemanly," her husband said, struggling internally. "People would say I was trying to influence him, or something. He'll come to visit us at home and identify - me."
[119] "How will he identify you?"
[120] "He's the only person in the world who truly understands me. You know my family is no longer alive, and most of the old servants have passed away, leaving only the nanny, who is in New Zealand. The current servant, Conos, has only been here for 10 years. I have many acquaintances, but you know I'm not very sociable and don't like making friends. Poor old crime investigator Murrey is undoubtedly the best choice, as he is impartial and has no connection with either side; however, if he intends to play the role of the great detective -"
[121] Lily took a deep breath. Her tanned, healthy face and robust physique made her blunt words all the more striking.
[122] "John, I don't understand. I really don't understand. You speak as if this were just a game of chance, 'not gentlemanly,' 'no ties to either side.' Do you realize that this person — whoever he is — has coldly claimed that he owns everything you have? That he is the real John Fairleigh? That he is the heir to the baronetcy and the £30,000 a year? That he intends to take everything from you?"
[123] "Yes, I understand that."
[124] "But don't you care at all?" Lily exclaimed. "You treat him so kindly and carefully, as if you don't care."
[125] "I care very much."
[126] "Exactly! If someone came to you and said, 'I am John Armin,' I believe you should say, 'Oh, really?' and then, without a word, kick him out or call the police. That's what I would do."
[127] "You don't understand the situation, dear. Barro said –"
[128] He slowly surveyed the room. He seemed to listen to the ticking of the clock, inhale the scent of the polished floor and spotless curtains, and traverse the sunlit expanse of land that now belonged to him. Curiously, at that moment, he appeared almost like a Puritan; also like a dangerous figure.
[129] "It would be a pity to lose all this now," he said slowly.
[130] The door opened, and he quickly composed himself, shifting from his quiet demeanor to a more attentive one. The balding old butler, Conyers, ushered in Nathaniel Barro and Brian Clegg.
[131] On the way here, Clegg noticed that Barlow was dressed in his most formal attire. He barely recognized him as the same man he had met earlier that afternoon. He thought perhaps it was necessary, as the atmosphere was too eerie: unlike anything he had ever experienced. He surveyed the host and hostess, beginning to regret coming here.
[132] The lawyer greeted the host and hostess with a nearly painful formality; Fairleigh stood upright, as if preparing for a duel.
[133] "I think," Barlow said, "that we should proceed with the matter at hand. Mr. Clegg has graciously agreed to serve as our witness."
[134] "Oh, I say," Clegg protested uneasily, "you know, we're not under siege. You're one of the most respected landowners in Kent. After hearing what Barlow told me," he looked at Fairleigh, feeling unable to continue the conversation, "it's as absurd as saying grass is red and water flows upwards. Most people would feel the same way. Do you really need to defend yourself so vehemently?"
[135] Fairleigh spoke slowly.
[136] "Yes, I was too stupid," he admitted.
[137] "You are stupid," Mei Li agreed, "Thank you, Brian."
[138] "Old Murray—" Fang Lei said, his gaze distant, "Have you seen him, Barrow?"
[139] "Just a brief meeting, Sir John, not an official one. The same with the opponent. In short, his stance is that he wants to conduct a test; he didn't say much else."
[140] "Has he changed a lot?"
[141] Barrow finally showed some humanity: "Not much. He's older, less flexible, and his temper has gotten worse; his beard is gray. Before -"
[142] "Before, right," Arlene mused, "I have a question for you. Have you ever suspected Muriel of being dishonest? Wait, that sounds strange. Old Muriel has always been too sincere, with nothing hidden; but we haven't seen him for 25 years, a long time. I've changed too, hasn't he? Will he play tricks?"
[143] "You can be assured of that," Barrow said solemnly. "I think we've discussed this already. I thought of it from the start. Are you satisfied with the sincerity of Mr. Muriel's actions?"
[144] "Yes, I think so."
[145] "So may I ask, why bring up this question at this time?"
[146] "You can help me," Fang Lei retorted, his attitude suddenly turning cold like Barrow's, "but you don't have to act like you think I'm a fraud or a villain. You do think that, don't deny it! That's exactly what you think. Calm down, calm down, calm down, I've searched the whole world just to find peace, where can I find it? Well, let me tell you why I asked about Muri. Since you haven't suspected that Muri would play tricks, why did you send a private detective to spy on him?"
[147] Barrow's eyes behind his large glasses widened in shock.
[148] "I apologize, Sir John. I did not hire a private detective to spy on Mr. Muri or anyone else."
[149] Fang Lei stood up. "Then who was that guy at the Bull's Head Inn? You know, the young man with a stern face, asking questions everywhere? Everyone in the village says he's a detective. He said he was interested in 'folklore' and was writing a book. Folklore, what a joke! He was like a leech sucking on Muri."
[150] Everyone exchanged glances.
[151] "Yes," Barro said thoughtfully, "I heard about the folklore researcher and his curious attitude towards the villagers. He might be sent by Wei Kai."

[1300] Chapter 14
[1301] The inquest into the death of Sir John Arbuthnot took place the following day, causing a stir among all British newspapers.
[1302] Detective Inspector Elliot, like most police officers, did not like inquests for practical reasons. Brian Clegg disliked inquests for aesthetic reasons: because you wouldn't make any new discoveries, because there were rarely interesting elements, and because any conclusions drawn, no matter what they were, wouldn't lead to new avenues of investigation.
[1303] However, he had to admit that the inquest held on Friday morning, July 31, did not follow the usual pattern. Although the suicide verdict was a foregone conclusion, the impassioned argument made by the first witness was truly impressive, and the result left Detective Inspector Elliot speechless.
[1304] Clegg drank strong black coffee for breakfast, grateful that they had not been questioned for yesterday afternoon's incident. Betty Hapgood had not died, but seeing the doll again had frightened her so much that she was still unable to speak. After that, Detective Inspector Elliot's endless questioning became listless. "Did you push it?" "I swear I didn't; I don't know who did; the floor of the attic is uneven, maybe nobody pushed it."
[1305] Last night, Detective Inspector Elliot and Dr. Philips smoked their pipes and drank beer while discussing the incident. Clegg drove Madeline home and made her eat something to calm her down after her shock, then he tried to sort through his thoughts while listening to the detective's opinions.
[1306] "This is a tricky situation," he said succinctly. "So far, nothing has been proven, but the case is growing like a snowball! Victoria Dare was murdered, possibly by the tramp, possibly not; let's not discuss the dirty details for now. That was a case over a year ago. Then Sir John Fairley was murdered by having his throat slashed. Betty Harper was then supposedly 'attacked' in the attic and carried downstairs, with her torn apron left on the floor of the library. The fingerprint record was stolen and then found shortly after. Finally, someone pushed the doll down the stairs, obviously intending to kill you, but you managed to escape by the skin of your teeth.
[1307] "Believe me, I'm grateful," Dr. Phil murmured uneasily. "When I looked up and saw the monster charging at me, it was the scariest moment of my life. It was my fault. I talked too much. But—"
[1308] Detective Elliot looked at him inquisitively. "Nevertheless, this proves that your line of reasoning is correct. The killer realized you knew too much. As for your line of reasoning, if you have any ideas, it's best to tell me now. You know, unless there's any progress in the case, I'll be called back to the city soon."
[1309] "Oh, I'll tell you soon," Dr. Phil grumbled. "I'm not being mysterious. Even if I tell you now, even if my reasoning is correct, it still proves nothing. Moreover, there's something I'm not sure about. Yes, I was scared. But I can't be sure that the doll was pushed downstairs to kill me."
[1310] "Then what was the purpose? It couldn't have been to scare the girl again. The killer couldn't have known it would happen to open the bedroom door."
[1311] "I know that," Dr. Phile insisted, rubbing his tangled gray hair. "But... but... evidence..."
[1312] "That's exactly what I mean. All these events have been happening one after another, but none of them can be proven! Nothing I can take to my superiors and say, 'That's the guy, go get him!' There's no evidence that holds up to scrutiny. I can't even prove that these events are connected, that's the crux of the matter. You must attend the inquest tomorrow, even though the police will undoubtedly rule it a suicide."
[1313] "Can't you postpone it?"
[1314] "Of course, I could. Usually, I would do so and indefinitely postpone it until we have concrete evidence of murder or the case is dropped. The problem is, since the suicide theory has been established, how can I further investigate the case? My inspector has almost concluded that John Fairley committed suicide, and the deputy minister is the same."
[1315] This was news to Basil, the final nail in the coffin of the suicide theory.
[1316] "No more games," Elliot confirmed his thoughts. "What else can I ask for?"
[1317] "What about Betty Harper?," Clegg asked.
[1318] "Well, even if she woke up and told the truth, what then? Even if she said who she saw in the library and what she did, how does that relate to the suicide in the garden? What can you prove, boy? The fingerprint record is the same, isn't it? That record never belonged to the deceased, so what's there to argue? No. Don't look at this case with intuition, sir, look at it legally. They may very well notify me soon, and close the case. You and I both know there's a murder here, clean enough to let the perpetrator go free unless someone stops it. But it looks like it's too late to stop it."
[1319] "What are you going to do?"
[1320] Elliot drank a pint of beer before answering, "I've said it, we only have one more chance: a real inquest. Most of our witnesses will be there to testify. The chances are slim, but maybe one of them will come clean under oath. I admit, the chances are small - but it's happened before, remember the Washington nurse's case? Maybe it will happen again. Since all other methods have failed, this is the police's last chance."
[1321] "Will the prosecutor cooperate with you?"
[1322] "It's difficult," Elliot said thoughtfully. "Barrow's playing games, I know that. But he won't come to me, and I can't find anything out from him. He went to the prosecutor. [1327] Cai Ji couldn't sleep all night. There were many miscellaneous tasks besides the questioning of the cause of death tomorrow. [1328] These things were churning up his sleep. The next day, the sun shone brightly and it was hot. At 9 o'clock, the vehicles began to rumble. He was furious. It didn't concern them at all, he thought. Why not just set up a swing and a carousel here and start selling hot dogs? [1329] Here, the residents do not make any comments on this matter in their daily lives, they do not take sides. In rural life, everyone always supports each other and both give and receive help. In a case like this, you must wait and see how things develop; things can be light and reasonable, regardless of the verdict. That beggar was from the Barry family? Just that beggar? He deserved it, and I'm sorry he didn't die again. From the pigsty, he came from there. But I don't care about him. I tell you, you can't harm my lady. She didn't kill him; she didn't; I can prove it."
[1968] In the silence, Dr. Phil's cane tapping the floor and his anxious breath could be heard. He approached Conrad, placing a hand on his shoulder.
[1969] "I know she didn't kill him," he said gently.

The only difference is that she didn't kill her husband with her own hands. She didn't operate the robot doll, and she wasn't the one hiding in the garden. [1987] Dear Dr. Phel:
[1988] Yes, I am the criminal. It was I who killed the imposter, and all the spooky tricks that have been bothering you were my doing.
[1989] I am writing this letter to you for several reasons. First: I have a genuine liking and respect for you, despite your stupidity. Second: you have done an excellent job. You forced me out of every room, out of every door, and finally out of my home to flee, and your methods have made me so impressed that I want to see if my actions are in line with your deductions. Allow me to flatter you: you are the only one who has outwitted me; however, I must admit that I am not good at opposing teachers.
[1990] I hope to receive a reply from you. By the time you receive this letter, my beloved Lily and I should be in a country with no extradition treaty with Great Britain. The country is quite hot, but Lily and I both love hot countries. Once we settle in our new home, I will send you a letter with our address.
[1991] I have a request. After our escape, in a series of embarrassing rumors, I will undoubtedly be portrayed as Satan, a monster, or a werewolf by newspapers, judges, and other media that distort the truth. However, you know I am not such a person. I do not have a preference for murder; however, if I do not feel any regret for the death of that beast, it is because - I hope - I am not a hypocrite. Some people are born different from others, like Lily and me. If we strive to make the world more interesting through our studies and daydreams, then I believe it will be a blessing for ordinary people and the beginning of the world's progress towards enlightenment. Therefore, if you hear anyone talking about Satan and his witch bride, please tell that person that you have had tea with us and know that we do not have horns or stigmata.
[1992] But now I must reveal my secret to you, which is also the secret of the case you are so eager to investigate. This secret is very simple and can be expressed in four words:
[1993] I have no legs.
[1994] I have no legs. My two legs were amputated in a small incident during the sinking of the Titanic in 1912 by that beast. I will explain the process later. Since then, the remarkable prosthetic legs I wear have probably not been able to successfully conceal my defect. I know you noticed my walking style - not exactly a limp, but always somewhat awkward and strange, and I often struggle to quicken my pace.
[1995] Have you ever thought about how artificial prosthetics can give an advantage when disguising oneself? We have wigs, fake beards, and makeup; we can change our appearance with clay and fillers; we can use subtle disguise techniques to perfection. However, there is one amazing fact I must point out, and that is that we have never used the easiest way to deceive the eyes - changing height. People often say, "One can change this and that, but one cannot disguise height." Allow me to correct myself: I can change my height at will, and I have been doing so for years.
[1996] I am not tall. To be more precise, if I could measure my original height, I would not be tall. Let's say that, if it were not for that little companion on the Titanic, I would be about 5 feet 5 inches tall.
[1997] After the amputation of my lower limbs (notice my euphemism), my actual height is less than 3 feet. If you have any doubts, simply measure your height against a wall and observe the proportion of the mysterious appendage we call legs.
[1998] I can order a few pairs of prosthetic legs - this was the first thing I did when I joined the circus - and then practice with these props, and I can choose any height I want. Interestingly, you will find that human eyes are so easily deceived. For example, imagine your small friend suddenly appearing in front of you as a 6-foot-tall person; your rational mind will reject this fact, and if you add a little more disguise, you will not recognize him at all.
[1999] I have changed my height several times. I was once 6 feet 1 inch tall. When I played the famous fortune-teller role of "Alimam," I was almost a dwarf: I disguised myself so well that when I later reappeared as Patrick Goyle in the presence of the kind-hearted Mr. H. Weikai, he was completely fooled.
[2000] Let me start by telling you about what happened on the Titanic a few days ago when I came to claim my inheritance. Everything I said in the study in front of everyone was true - except for a slight distortion and an obvious omission.
[2001] As I said, we did indeed exchange identities. As I said, the soft-hearted little thing did indeed try to kill me. He just tried to strangle me because he was taller and stronger than me. This little comedy of errors took place in the most tragic of circumstances; you guessed the setting. It was a massive white-painted stainless steel door, a watertight compartment door, that turned a passenger liner into many separate rooms, with its hundreds of pounds of metal doors to withstand the seawater that was constantly seeping in. But when the ship suddenly tilted, the hinges on the door were crushed and split, and I think I've never seen a more terrifying sight; it felt like all order in the world had suddenly collapsed, or the gates of Gath had collapsed in the Old Testament.
[2002] My little friend didn't ask for much. He choked me into unconsciousness and then wanted to lock me in that watertight compartment and escape. I resisted, looking for anything within reach - discovering a wooden mallet hanging on the door. I don't remember how many times I hit him, but the son of the snake charmer seemed not to care at all. I tried to squeeze through the door - unfortunately, at this time the son of the snake charmer pushed the door with his body, combined with the swaying of the ship, the hinges of the door fell off. As a result, my body - except for my legs - was completely severed.
[2003] It was an era filled with heroes, doctor, and heroic deeds rarely praised or celebrated. Who saved me - whether a passenger or crew member - I don't know. I only remember being picked up like a puppy and put on a small boat. As for the bloody-handed, dazed-eyed son of the snake charmer, I guess he probably perished. As for how I managed to survive, I think I owe it to the saltwater, but those days were difficult to bear until a week later when I remembered these things.
[2004] A few days ago, in the story I told in the garden of the Arum House, I mentioned my name "Patrick Gall," which was taken by the late circus manager, Boris Yeltsin. I also briefly explained my state of mind at the time. If I didn't fully explain my state of mind, you should understand the reason. Boris quickly found a use for me in the circus because I was a (don't misunderstand my self-promotion) deformed monster and possessed fortune-telling skills I had acquired from reading in my hometown. It was a very painful and humiliating time. Especially having to learn to "walk" with only my hands. I don't want to spend time describing this part because I don't want you to think I'm begging for pity or sympathy: just thinking about it makes me angry. I can accept your respect, or kill you. As for your pity? Go to hell!
[2005] This also suddenly reminds me that I have been putting on airs, shaping myself into a tragic figure by recalling some forgotten past events. So let's take a lighter look at
[2006] I am eagerly assuring you that being without legs has actually been a blessing for my career, though I had no choice. However, prosthetics have always been a hindrance; I dare say I never really learned to use them properly. I learned to move my body with my hands earlier, and I can confidently say that it was fast and flexible. As you can imagine, this has been quite beneficial for my psychic medium career and has allowed me to create numerous fascinating effects for the audience. Think about it, and you'll understand.
[2007] Whenever I use such techniques, I usually wear closed-mouth shorts with rubber pads sewn into the legs of my prosthetic and pants, which act as my legs and leave no trace on any kind of ground. Due to the importance of quick dressing, I have also mastered the ability to remove or attach my prosthetic within 35 seconds.
[2008] Of course, this is also the simple yet incredibly awkward secret that I am able to operate that robot doll.
[2009] As the saying goes, history repeats itself. This kind of thing not only could have happened, but it did happen. You know, Doctor, this is how the chess-playing automaton of Crompton and Mager was operated? By having someone like me as an assistant, entering the base box of the automaton, it managed to deceive Europe and America for 50 years. Since even Napoleon and P.T. Barnum, such great figures, couldn't see through it, you shouldn't feel bad if it fooled you too. In fact, it didn't fool you; I understood it from the code you gave me in the attic.
[2010] I am quite certain that this is the secret of the 17th-century golden witch. Now you should understand why my esteemed ancestor Thomas Foley paid a fortune for it and discovered this secret, causing the robot doll to be abandoned ever since. They revealed the inner workings of this thing to him, and like all those who discovered this secret, he was enraged. He thought he had acquired a treasure. Who would have thought that he had spent his money on a clever deception, unless he could find a person of special stature to operate it, which would then be able to deceive his friends.
[2011] The original operation of the doll was as follows: As you have seen, for someone like me, the space inside is large enough. Once you enter the "sofa" or the base box of the doll and close the door, a small window opens at the top of the box that controls the doll's movements. Inside this box - a simple mechanical principle - there are about ten rods connected to the doll's arms and body. The doll's knees have hidden holes that can be opened from the inside, allowing the operator to see outside. This is how the chess-playing doll by Mager understood chess; the Golden Witch of over a hundred years ago was also able to play the sitar due to this principle.
[2012] However, the Golden Witch's appeal lies in the fact that the operator hidden inside the box can remain unseen. I believe this is where the inventor of the Golden Witch, Kemble, outdid himself. At the beginning of the performance, the magician opens the box to let the audience see that it is empty. So, how did the operator get inside?
[2013] I don't need to tell you. Based on what you said about the performer's costume the day after the incident - carefully directed at me - I realize you know it all too well; this gives me goosebumps.
[2014] It is well known that a magician's traditional costume includes a long robe covered in hieroglyphics. The inventor of the Golden Witch borrowed this principle - a principle later adopted by poor Indian magicians - was to use this robe to deceive people. For Indian magicians, it was to let a child crawl into a hidden basket; for the Golden Witch, it was to let the operator enter the machine during the magician's moment of darkness when he was playing with the robe. I have successfully used this trick in many performances.

Her devil-worshipping tendencies are not the cold-hearted mental activity of a woman who enjoys seeing others' souls corrupted; her nature is not cold, and you know that very well. She engages in those activities because she enjoys them. I believe she will always enjoy them. To make it sound as if she murdered Victoria Durracq is absurd; the life of that woman living near Tunbridge Wells is so mysterious and inscrutable that it cannot be corroborated or prosecuted. I admit that there is a lower aspect to her nature; I admit that in myself; but beyond that? Our removal from Kent and England is not the end of a moral drama. It is rather like an ordinary family moving to the seaside in a state of confusion, the father forgetting where he has put his ticket, and the mother worrying about whether the oil lamp in the old house's bathroom has been extinguished. I imagine that Adam and Eve left the broad garden in a similar atmosphere of panic; and this, like Alice would also agree, is the supreme law of the book as stated by the King.

[2063] Translated text: Phineas Tailor Barnum was a famous showman and manager of the 19th century, specializing in organizing unusual shows and exhibitions.​
[2064] Translated version: In the story of Alice's Adventures in Wonderland, the King established Rule 42 in the rulebook, commanding all individuals taller than 1 mile to leave the court. Alice is two miles tall and refuses to leave, to which the King tells her, "This is the highest law in my book."​
